B0000C8AW2|5.0|In the fall of 2005, WXPN (88.5 FM), the public radio station from the University of Pennsylvania, did a Greatest 885 Albums of All Time countdown, based on listeners votes.  We voted for our 10 favorite albums.  My #1?  Street Legal.   To put this into context - I've been a huge fan of Dylan's since the fall of 1964 when I first heard Bringing it All Back Home in a college dorm room.  Among my other favorites - Blood on the Tracks, Love and Theft, Another Side Of, Blonde on Blonde, New Morning, Saved, lately a lot of the live ones...well, most all of them are great.  So, what is it that makes Street Legal special?   Since favorites choices are just about what you like, in this case what you find yourself wanting to listen to again more than anything else, I could just say \\"Don't really know\\" and get out of here.  But how much fun would that be? <br /> <br /> On the surface of things, the musical choices I love - the horns and backup singers (from the echoed \\"sixteen years\\"  at the start, to the \\"hey, hey, heys\\" at the end) are great.   Dylan can express amazing stuff with his voice alone, but adding this fullness to the mix is like watching Picasso go from blue to the full palette.   As to  the whole Dylan's trying to go Las Vegas Revue theory - its pretty clear that whatever resemblances to that form are there (including that funny photo on the back) are satirical, a Dylan staple from the get go.  And the music is hardly smooth Vegas big band stuff - its funky, bluesy, gospely, loose Dylan music. <br /> <br /> One of the patterns I see over the course of Dylan's career is kind of a slow orbit (to allude to the line \\"drifting, like a satellite\\") from close to the sun of the mainstream, where he makes an album targeted for commercial success and a new shot of \\"critics grace\\", maybe putting himself in the hands of a producer with a distinctive sound (Mark Knopfler or Daniel Lanois), getting serious, so to speak, then moving out into the deep space of his own instincts, exploring, and letting his own natural reactions to the times and the stage of his career guide him.   Moving from the structure of folkie political songs on The Times They Are a Changin' to the inner poetry on Another Side; from the end of the epoch defining, symbolist poetry spewing, early rock star period of Blonde on Blonde to the spare, biblical, apocalyptic feel of John Wesley Harding;  from the clean folk blues stories of personal struggle on Blood on the Tracks, through Desire to ... whatever it is that is Street Legal. <br /> <br /> That's as close as I can come, and why I love it.   To me, its the hardest to put your finger on, with the most fully \\"Dylan-realized\\" batch of songs he's ever put together.   I still wouldn't know how to classify Changing of the Guards and No Time to Think as types of songs.   The latter flirts with being \\"classical\\".    I wonder if the relative failure of the album with the critics and the public turned Dylan away from further attempts at these longer, more complex melody phrases.   If so, too bad, cause he mastered the form like he did all of the others he tried over the years.  And, as someone else pointed out in one of these reviews, this is the last album before the Christian trilogy - when perhaps the inner life was most inchoate, when the need to stretch to make the music organize the chaos the greatest.   To my ear, that's great art lurking within that Vegas lounge act. <br /> <br /> OK, this is long enough, and I need to do some work.  Just wanted to say something about my favorite album.    Last thought - what might be next?   If history holds then the Time Out of Mind period should be about over, with Modern Times.    I say to Bob, go back to Street Legal and do your Love and Theft thing on your own roots - lets have some more of that big band, quasi-classical, who knows what it is stuff for the new millenium.

The on location shots in Spain on the open seas are fabulous! It adds a huge sense of reality to the whole movie. Lighting and the music is just beautiful too.<br /><br />The extra features... yes, I'm such a fanatic, the minute I checked the mail I put the DVD in & listened to the commentary by Stamp & Soderbergh. If you are looking to find out a lot about the movie, you may be disappointed- if you wanted to find out a lot about Terrance Stamp, you'll be happy. He speaks at length about his own career and some things unrelated to the movie. There is not a lot of scene by scene commentary. He does speak about Ustinov and some of the things about production. His discourse is erudite and even occasionally stimulating. Soderbergh is in the role of an interviewer in this case. I enjoyed the commentary, but one does wonder how much it would've been enriched if someone had gotten off their dead butts & done this before Ustinov died. (I mourn him openly- he was my renaissance man- funny, smart and a humanitarian.)<br /><br />All and all, I'm quite pleased to have this DVD. I'm thankful to Warner Bros. for putting it out on DVD with a quality picture and sound.<br /><br />HIGHLY RECOMMENDED!

I'm not saying that I share the same beliefs as either Luther or Morse--I'm not even of the Christian faith for that matter--but I do believe that Luther's anger was justified and that it's a compelling story to be told.<br /><br />So, why did I feel the need to say all that? 0440180295|5.0|Billy Pilgrim is unstuck in time, and so the novel is also unstuck from the shackles of linear time progression.  Throughout the work, Vonnegut explores the modern world, World War II Dresden as a prisoner of the Germans, and the planet Tralfamador where Billy is displayed like a zoo animal.  Everything is happening at once, so we get snippets from various points of time and the plot lines are disconnected.<br /><br />Through this narrative, Vonnegut works his themes like a virtuoso.  He shows that war can do nothing but destroy, that nothing flowers from the remains of war except a shell of what could've been if there'd been no massive deaths, no societal collapse, no degradations and dehumanizations.  He lives through Billy Pilgrim as a witness to the firebombing of Dresden, through the lives lost to a greater degree than Hiroshima or Nagasaki.  He shows that horror so vividly that it's the very horror of that experience that tosses him about time and space like a pinball in a game.<br /><br />He rips through the concept of free will, when Billy decides he can just change history and prevent war.  He shows the futility, illustrating it with this great line, \\"writing an anti-war book is like writing an anti-glacier book.\\"  Billy can't simply ignore war, and his sanity erodes as a consequence.  Everyone should just ignore war is the advice of the Tralfamadorians, who have their own history of wars, once even destroying the whole universe.  But Billy is human and can't understand the circular nature of time.<br /><br />Vonnegut doesn't so much write a novel as have a heart-felt conversation with the reader, throwing in some fiction, some religion, some politics, and a laugh or two. After reading this book, you'll have new understandings, and a great new friend.<br /><br /> - CV Rick

B00005JPG1|4.0|\\"Zodiac\\" is the most brilliantly uncreative movie of David Fincher's career. For a popular director of such renowned innovation, Fincher has once again avoided all expectations with a film unimaginably straightforward in execution. This may sound like sarcasm, but it is not. I came into \\"Zodiac\\" wondering how Fincher would use his usual visual flair in a film tackling actual events, events from which some still hold wounds. I was shocked to find that Fincher had dumped the usual tricks (imaginative credit sequences, CGI bolstered visuals, editing that makes MTV jealous) in place of a very straightforward approach to the material. By doing so Fincher has allowed an extremely riveting and compelling story to become a riveting and compelling film, and has put to shame those numerous generic Hollywood directors who seem unable to make genuinely entertaining mid-budget genre movies anymore. \\"Zodiac\\" is better than their films because Fincher knows his story, knows his characters, and knows the exact tone and style with which to present them.<br /><br />The story is easily the most important ingredient of \\"Zodiac\\". First of all, everyone loves a good serial killer movie. Add to that the fact that this is a true serial killer story, taking place over many years and involving numerous interesting people, and you can't go wrong. Plus it has never been solved, an intriguing bonus. The filmmakers have obvious gone to great pains to research this film, and their understanding of the events and players involved really shines through and carries the tone of the film. Accuracy is certainly attempted, in terms of the catalogued facts, and even where liberties have been taken, in terms of depicting events and conversations impossible to recreate exactly, it seems that nothing completely unsubstantiated has been pawned off as fact. The movie is still just a movie, and the idea of `accurately' portraying any actual event is always questionable, but the filmmakers here seem to understand that the greatest disservice they could have done to the material would have been to falsely embellish it. The story is interesting enough, and the people involved compelling enough, that to try to make the film an exercise in cinematic enhancement would have seemed cheap and inappropriate.<br /><br />And Fincher, the king of cinematic enhancement, shows surprising talent in pulling this off. The story is told in a very straightforward manner. There is little fancy camerawork, the editing is noticeable only in how well it handles the scattered chronology of the story, the actors are allowed to use talent alone to get across their characters, and the film itself is astonishingly uncomplicated. There are a few creative moments, such as a time lapsed sequence of a building being built, but even these are integrated so well into the general tone and pace of the film that they are hardly noticeable. This is a key word, because Fincher has always had, or lately anyways, a very noticeable touch as a director, whereas here, if you did not know who directed, or who David Fincher is, you wouldn't think twice about the direction while watching. Which is the film's greatest success, the fact that it utilizes and relies on its compelling story to drive the film, and doesn't try to use the film to make the story compelling. Does that make sense? The ability to simply tell a great story is still the most important talent needed for a great director, a talent not readily found, and Fincher here has proven that even without his usual bag of tricks he is still a extremely able director.<br /><br />\\"Zodiac\\" isn't a masterpiece, or even Fincher's best film, but it is one of the best mid-budget crime films I've seen in some time. It knows just what it is trying to be and do, it concentrates on doing that well, and pulls it off with great success. It's actively compelling in a way few Hollywood films manage and its story is one that will continue to intrigue for some time to come. David Fincher has once again, and in a very different way, proven himself an exceptionally gifted director.ome. David Fincher has once again, and in a very different way, proven himself an exceptionally gifted director.
